B000002JEV|4.0|This two disc set captures Zeppelin in its early stages of prominence as one of the most significant British Rock bands of its time. Disc One features performances as early as March of '69 and then 3 times during June of that year and while some tracks are repeated(You Shook Me and I Can't Quit You Baby), its interesting to hear how the performances differ and grow from a period of a few months time. The appearance of Communication Breakdown 3 times is a bit much(although this is probably due to the fact that it was a featured single from the debut album), and is a bit irritating after repeated spins and makes one wonder if there were other tracks that could have been used instead. There are a few rarities such as Eddie Cochran's Somethin Else, the Sleepy John Estes track, The Girl I Love(by which the main riff would constitute Moby Dick, Bonham's showcase) and Robert Johnson's Travelling Riverside Blues, which are a pleasure to have within the wealth of this set.<br /><br />Disc two is taken from a sole performance at London's Paris Theatre in April of '71, by which time the group would still be several months away from the release of the seminal 4th album, one gets to hear versions of Black Dog, Stairway to Heaven and Going To California in their pristine live forms. There are also versions from the III album of one of my favorite Blues homages Since I've Been Loving You, the folksy That's the Way, and the driving, propelling Immigrant Song. Dazed and Confused is updated and shows how significant the number's duration would become a tour-de-force for Jimmy Page's guitar workouts in the live performances over the next four years, leading way to the infamous violin bow schtick that attached itself to Page's growing guitar god status. Further featured are versions from the II album in Heartbreaker, an enlongated Whole Lotta Love replete with a 4 song medley including Boogie Chillin(John Lee Hooker) and Elvis' That's All Right Mama; Thank You is included and closes out the disc with a performance that is strong and noteworthy for this seldom live performed number.<br /><br />The BBC sessions is a pleasure to own for the hardcore fan base, while also can be slightly of importance to the casual fan if not for the inclusions of songs like Black Dog, What is and What Should Never Be and Stairway to Heaven. What with the limited live releases available, BBC sessions comes up wanting in a flush of major importance to the Zeppelin collector. 4 stars

B000002JSP|3.0|Recorded in Stockholm at Abba's Polar Studios in late 1978, Zeppelin's eighth studio(and last) release finds the reins being held most significantly by John Paul Jones and Robert Plant. Although Page's presence is undoubtably present and accounted for, the sounds of the group as a whole on this record, are at a much more mature and relaxed nature. Which isn't to say that the power of the group isn't still there, opening cut In the Evening being a prime example which is driven by Page's repetitive melody riff and Bonham's driving beats, but one can hear the obvious changes in the band's approach to the finished tracks chosen and overall lighter sense of ensemble perfomance and songwriting quality. Fool in the Rain has a bit of a lazy calypso-styled rhythm which is anchored by Bonham's kit playing while Robert's earnest vocal matches the song's commercial-like qualities. Jones accentuates the track during its tail end-samba flavored bridge with tack piano and caribbean timbales being used for further texture culminating in a track that is unique and fresh. South Bound Saurez is also driven by Jones' rattling, boogie piano melodies while Page offers a blistering solo during the duration of this relaxed and fun little number.<br /><br />Hot Dog features Page tackling a slight country western/boogie feel(aka chicken pickin') along with Jones providing yet again, strong boogie woogie style piano accompaniment. Plant's vocal range on this has a bit of homage to Elvis Presley in his tonality and phrasings which further add to the quirky fun of this odd, but entertaining number. The last 3 tracks in Carouselambra, All My Love and the blues-dirge I'm Gonna Crawl are driven by Jones' synthesizer arrangements and further shows an updated sound for the impending 80's decade to come. Carouselambra is the longest cut on the record, and is a personal favorite of mine as it features alot of trademark Zeppelin styles with a myriad of tempo and chord changes, as well as displaying thesonic qualities of musicianship amongst the core of the band members, esp. Jones' synth lines dominating the bulk of the track. All My Love is the ballad of the record and would also be another commercially driven track that stands out as among the most sentimental of Zeppelin's output. During the middle of the track, there is Page's layering of acoustic and electric guitars split amongst Jones' synth arrangements, while Plant's vocal is displayed at his most heartfelt and vulnerable perfomance yet. I'm Gonna Crawl hears the band returning to the blues again for inspiration, even though this number is probably one of the more weaker moments on here, this time around, the band fails to truly come up with something of sustainable substance here, despite the promise of strength throughout the variedness of the musical landscape displayed thus far, Crawl is apt in its title and perfomance.<br /><br />Door is a 3 star effort overall, but its a solid 3 star rating to my ears, as it captures a once powerful and innovative band in its ultimate twilight moment.

Then again, you can probably make that inference just from the album name itself. It should also come as no surprise that the content of \\"My December\\" follows suit with the album cover. There is no light this time around, there are none of the glossy pop hits that turned Kelly into a success star, there are no indellible hooks that make you want to repeatedly listen to this set. Quite frankly, there's not a whole lot of anything here. Even though Kelly has such an incredible voice, and she took the reins of her music and co-wrote every song, she does not utilize either of those talents, even though this was supposed to be the defining moment in her career when she finally comes into her own.<br />But it didn't have to turn out like this. Kelly, I hate to say it, but you really should have listen to Clive Davis this time. Even though this album is the most personal Kelly has been with the mainstream, it's not the side that people want to listen to. But at the same time, I'll admit that this is both an artistic and personal change in sound that she needed to make. If she didn't get all of her emotions out on this CD, then her fans would have to endure multiple albums of her trying to be a hard-rock girl and singing about nothing but break-ups and depression. She wanted this album to be comparable to \\"Evanescence\\", but it ends up sounding like a tiring and dry version of Avril Lavigne's \\"Under My Skin\\". However, the bright side of this dark album, is now that Kelly has aired her dirty laundry, a much better album surely awaits.

0486268659|5.0|More than sixty years ago I read my first book by Charles Dickens: A Christmas Carol. Mesmerized by the three ghosts, the stinginess of Ebenezer Scrooge, and the frailty of Tiny Tim, I kept the book beside my bed, tucked under the lamp on the night stand close at hand in the family home in Iowa.  When the situation seemed unbearable within the walls where the family quarreled daily, I would read it, and imagine myself freed from the hatred around me.<br /><br />When I began teaching at a small rural Iowa college. I risked everything to introduce A Christmas Carol to my students as representative of 19th century England: the work houses, the potato famine and hunger, the miserliness of those with financial means to stay aloof from the ever-increasing poor. Reading Dickens' Christmas Carol enabled many to discover that life had not changed that much.  At night, when I would return to my small apartment on the second floor of an old wooden house, buffeted by harsh winds and empty space, prepared a cup of potato soup or wheat gruel for supper, I began to feel myself to be an ever increasing part of Dickens' tale.<br /><br />Later when I taught at major universities in the USA and abroad, the Dickens classic was my constant companion. When I taught English history or English literature it was required reading.  Discussions turned to interpretation and the book's relationship to the present clime and time: we went through the counting houses of banks, the dark alleys where the gin-soaked poor brought stolen goods to those who would resell, and the holes where prostitutes earned a scant living, much as occurs today. Of course I encouraged my students to also read Hard Times, Oliver Twist and other Dickens classics to discover the misery of the Industrial Age and the scorn the rich had for the poor, outsourcing jobs, lending money at usurious rates, and the calloused treatment of employees. Things had not changed much, and personal choice was only for the wealthy who counted for about 1% of the people.<br /><br />At sixty I moved to one of the poorest, most heavily impoverished nations where crime is a daily routine, education at best a joke, and plagiarism expected and condoned by teachers at all levels. In 2007, after Antonio Chang enforced the law to test all teachers in Peru, when I learned that only 151 out of more than 185,000 teachers who were tested for basic compentency had passed, I offered to teach the teachers. My first book of choice was A Christmas Carol as it covers every art and literary aspect of dialogue and education. I am still teaching the teachers--the few who allow me the honor, while most prefer to stay at home or travel to a second and/or third school to teach for additional income.  I am still using Charles Dickens' stellar work. I pull out my weathered copy of A Christmas Carol and read it to my students and collegues, and then show the George C. Scott rendition of the classic that is now on DVD and fortunately is available at Amazon.com. Several students swiftly see the similarity between 19th century London and contemporary Peru where survival remains the primary goal and the only source of hope was within the walls of churches. Unfortunately the 1% of Peru who own the means of production and wealth have not yet met the Ghost of Christmas Past and the clergy they sit in front of like marble statues are more like Dostoyovsky's Grand Inquistor in his classic The Brothers Karamazov than the pastor in Dickens' Christmas Carol.<br /><br />A Christmas Carol is mandatory reading in my English classes. Passing any course I teach requires a demonstrative ability to show the relevance of the Dickens' classic about Ebeneezer Scrooge to the twenty-first century elites who still rule over lives and destinies.<br /><br />Today the students at my university in the north of Peru, are working to help the very poor nestled on a small hill in front of the great gates to this privately-owned center of learning to plan for running water and a sewer, to go to school, to look for better jobs, and improve their lot in life. The wasteland that many have moved to in order to escape the depression in the mountains is but a mirror of the rampant poverty of the shanties surrounding Lima, but in each case A Christmas Carol's presentation of the poor districtions of London are easily understood by the poor of Peru, and Ebeneezer's epiphany is readily known and hoped for as a better future for all.  Peru has a long way to go before it will climb out of the abyss it is in because of a too-long history of rule by dictators and teachers who occupied classrooms for the limited money but without any skills to impart knowledge, A Christmas Carol gives hope to those who seek a better life, and the Ghost of Christmas past still has its finger pointing at marked graves at those who have no charity, no compassion,no sense of feeling.  Dickens' tale will help change that and I will teach it as long as there is breath in this old body.

B003WUYOQ6|4.0|Taubes makes a convincing argument that is very well documented and backed by sound science, for how carbohydrates and specifically starches and sugars result in hormonal imbalances leading to obesity and other diseases. I highly recommend this book for anyone who wants meticulous, scientific coverage of the subject of obesity, nutrition, and human biology. Not only does he make a sound argument in favor of his position but shows with ample scientific evidence that the low-fat, plant-based diets we are all told to eat are bunk.<br /><br />While this book is quite excellent, I must bump it one star for the rather non-scientific way in which his sources are cited. As a scientist myself I expect specific references to the primary literature and easy access to those citations, e.g. via endnotes or footnotes. Instead, all the sources are itemized in a single chapter at the end, listed by chapter used. Without endnotes, it is difficult to specifically associate each claim he makes with a particular source. Nevertheless, the sources are there and are valid, and the book is very well researched. I highly recommend the book.

0374249512|5.0|Having never heard of Fred Ulhman, I was first intrigued by the foreword by Arthur Koestler, who called it a minor masterpiece - a description in which I wholely concur. In novella form, not much longer than a short story (only 100 pages), it describes the close friendship and subsequent alienation of two young boys, one a Jew, the other an aristocrat, during one of the pre-war years in Germany at the time of the rise of Hitler and his Nazi followers. It is beautifully written, although English is not the native tongue of Ulhman, a German Jew who was living in England at the time this was written. One has the feeling that this book is almost autobiographical.

B00729GQ0A|5.0|I very much enjoyed both Rebecca Hamilton's writing style and the well-crafted fantasy world she has put together in this debut novel.  The description is crisp and vivid, drawing clear images of the small mountain town where most of the story takes place.  The main character Sophia has a consistent, enjoyable voice that is sometimes witty but avoids being overly snarky, while also having enough vulnerability to make the reader feel sympathy for her.  There's plenty of spooky action throughout, along with an original and fascinating cast of mythical creatures that goes far beyond the usual vampire story.  The author put a lot of thought into creating a complex fantasy realm in which there is more to discover on every page, and the storyline fits together impressively well.<br /><br />The romance builds slowly, but its pace suits Sophia's defensiveness and awkward innocence.  Midway through the story there's a sudden plot twist that is very effective; without posting any spoilers, I'll say that I never saw it coming, and the story segues smoothly into another character's perspective with some historical background too.  It's not easy for a writer to pull that off effectively.  The fast-paced ending builds up the creepiness level so much that, for the last few chapters, the book just can't be put down!  Looking forward to more from this author.

If you like, he presents a rational way to deal with irrationality. 0393338398|4.0|Michael Lewis is a national treasure.  He thinks in an original way on a wide variety of topics, and then puts it in print in a readable way.  He has illuminated complex topics for me in many books, and has done so again in Moneyball.  In high school in the 1970s, my friends and I played a lot of \\"Superstar Baseball\\", a board game with complicated dice which lent itself to statistical analysis.  We were surprised that the real life statistics of the players represented did not always match well with that of the board game, and many of us took advantage of that in constructing our teams.  This is basically what Billy Beane, the main character of the book, is shown to do.  Lewis is most passionate in his defense of statistics against \\"experts\\" with received wisdom whose wisdom is generally inconsistent with data and uninformed by an understanding of probability theory.  I read this just after Daniel Kahneman's \\"Thinking, Fast and Slow\\", and the two books complement each other nicely.  Kanheman's thesis is, in condensed version, that we use our reflexive instincts to make too many decisions, to our detriment.  He backs it up with data and a recent Nobel Prize.  Lewis' book appears to be a prime example demonstrating this thesis.  My only reason for 4 stars is that at times I found the writing style a little jumpy, but this is a small criticism.
